    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="fab06444ef274f57ad39d7a16ab8c0e0">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '84b45cfc-adda-4de5-9748-5534647b76a8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d238440641" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Titanium disulfide (TiS<sub>2</sub>) is investigated as an advanced conversion electrode for sodium (Na)-ion batteries (NIB) in an ether-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/glyme (DME)). The as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 1040 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> with the capacity contribution of 521 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage region below 1.0 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), remarkable initial coulombic efficiency of 95.9% and superior rate capability of 621 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The high conductivity of the Ti-based compounds and nanosized particles generated by chemical conversion reactions could minimize the entropic barrier for the reversible conversion, resulting in high reversibility and ultrafast charge/discharge ability of the electrode. Moreover, with its strong ability to adsorb soluble polysulfide intermediates, the as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode exhibits superior cycling stability over 9000 cycles, serving as a stable and ultra-high capacity conversion electrode for NIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs798-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs798-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rapid growth of the need for renewable and sustainable energy in the global energy market has inspired an urgent demand for the development of large-scale energy storage systems with low cost and high efficiency.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0001_R_d238440625e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0003_R_d238440625e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">3</a></span> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have successfully dominated the portable electronic market in the past decades, their large-scale application in electric vehicles and smart grid still face significant problems due to the high cost and restricted lithium source. As the most promising alternative, Na-ion batteries (NIBs) have recently attracted a highly revived interest owing to the widespread sodium (Na) reserves and low environmental impact.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0004_R_d238440625e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">4</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0008_R_d238440625e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Sci.">8</a></span> However, the larger ionic radius of the Na<sup>+</sup> ion hinders the electrochemical reaction kinetics and imposes difficulties with respect to exploiting appropriate electrodes with high capacities, fast charge–discharge properties, and long-cycle lives.</p>
         
         <p>Layered transition-metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) have been extensively investigated as energy storage materials owing to their robust structure, low cost, and environmental friendliness.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0009_R_d238440625e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">9</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0016_R_d238440625e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></span> The unique open framework of TMDs guarantees acceptable mobility of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/desertion and diffusion. Moreover, they can deliver attractive high theoretical capacities (895 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for FeS<sub>2,</sub> 558 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for CuS, etc.) through multistep reaction mechanisms, including intercalation and conversion reactions. In general, the latter reaction induces severe volume change in the electrode materials during cycling, resulting in drastic structural collapse and rapid capacity decay. By controlling the cut-off voltage to restrain the detrimental conversion reactions, the cycling performance of TMDs could be remarkably improved.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0016_R_d238440625e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></span> However, the inferior reversible capacity based on the intercalation reaction is far from what is expected.</p>
         
         <p>The key aspect for achieving high electrochemical performances from the TMDs is to sustain the high reversibility of the conversion reactions, in which the electrolyte plays a significant role. Recent studies reveal that conventional ester-based electrolytes are not suitable for most TMDs since the solvents would provoke side reactions with the anionic group of polysulfide. An ether-based electrolyte with higher chemical stability is a better choice to ensure long-term cycling stability.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0012_R_d238440625e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0016_R_d238440625e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></span> Furthermore, compared to intercalation or alloying reactions, the kinetics of the conversion reactions are extremely sluggish owing to the complex reaction mechanisms involving structural decomposition and reconstruction. To improve the capacity utilization and rate capability of TMDs, intensive efforts have been aimed at developing ultrafine nanoparticles so as to minimize the Na<sup>+</sup> pathways and create abundant electrochemically active nanodomains for efficient and reversible reactions.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0017_R_d238440625e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></span> However, the cycling performance of TMDs are still unsatisfactory, which can be ascribed to the dissolution of the polysulfide in the ether-based electrolyte during the conversion reaction.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0014_R_d238440625e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a></span> Moreover, the complicated synthetic route, low compacted density, and poor initial coulombic efficiency of the nanostructured materials are not desirable for practical large-scale applications.</p>
         
         <p>To achieve significant breakthrough in the electrochemical performances of TMDs, it should be emphasized on developing strategies for suppressing the dissolution of polysulfide intermediates generated during the conversion reactions. Titanium disulfide (TiS<sub>2</sub>), with its ability to adsorb polysulfide, is an attractive candidate for Na storage.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0018_R_d238440625e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nanotechnology">18</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0021_R_d238440625e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">21</a></span> In a recent work, Takeuchi and co-workers have proven that TiS<sub>2</sub> strongly interacts with polysulfide intermediates and can efficiently enhance the electrochemical performances of Li–S batteries by suppressing the polysulfide dissolution.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0020_R_d238440625e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">20</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0021_R_d238440625e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">21</a></span> In addition, TiS<sub>2</sub> is a well-known semimetal used as the first generation cathode in metal ion batteries, with favorable intercalation kinetics.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0022_R_d238440625e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Ionics">22</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0023_R_d238440625e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A">23</a></span> Recently, Chaturvedi et al. prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> crystalline platelets via chemical vapor transport and evaluated it as an insertion host for NIBs. It delivers a reversible capacity of 146 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C (1 C = 239 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) with a stable cycling performance over 160 cycles.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0024_R_d238440625e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ChemistrySelect">24</a></span> Li and co-workers reported TiS<sub>2</sub> nanoplates with a considerable Na-storage capacity of 186 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> based on the intercalation reaction, demonstrating a capacity retention of 76% over 300 cycles.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0025_R_d238440625e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></span> To the best of our knowledge, the electrochemical conversion reaction of TiS<sub>2</sub> has never been investigated in LIBs or NIBs. In fact, TiS<sub>2</sub> has the lowest weight among all the TMDs, which can nearly facilitate a four-electron transfer through electrochemical conversion of TiS<sub>2</sub> to metallic Ti, with a theoretical capacity of 957 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. In this contribution, we investigate the electrochemical conversion reactions of TiS<sub>2</sub> in NIBs using an ether-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/glyme (DME)). Compared to the intercalation mechanism previously reported, the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode based on the conversion reactions demonstrate greatly improved Na-storage performances in terms of reversible capacity, cycling stability, and rate capability. Benefiting from the high electronic conductivity of TiS<sub>2</sub> and its strong affinity for the soluble polysulfide, the as-prepared electrode demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 1040 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, strong rate capability of 621 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and superior cycling stability over 9000 cycles.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs798-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs798-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>TiS<sub>2</sub> has a layered structure analogous to graphite, with the Ti ions sandwiched between two packed sulfur layers, and these are held closely together by van der Waals forces (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs798-fig-0001">1</a>a). The crystal structure of as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> is shown in Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0001">1</a>b. Except for few peaks of S which cannot be washed away by CS<sub>2</sub>, all the diffraction peaks can be well indexed to a hexagonal structure (standard JCPDS No. 15-0853) with the space group P3̄ml (164).<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0018_R_d238440625e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nanotechnology">18</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0022_R_d238440625e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Ionics">22</a></span> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to investigate the elemental bonding configurations of as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub>. The high-resolution Ti 2p XPS spectrum (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0001">1</a>c) exhibits two peaks at 458.8 and 464.6 eV ascribed to Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p3/2 and Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p1/2, respectively.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0026_R_d238440625e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Nanopart. Res.">26</a></span> The peaks at the binding energies of 160.8, 162, and 163.3 eV in the high-resolution S 2p spectrum indicate the presence of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>S and S<span class="icomoon"></span>S covalent bonds (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0001">1</a>d). The mass content of S is calculated to be 5.9% based on the XPS analysis and TG curve (Figure S1, Supporting Information). In the Raman spectrum (Figure S2, Supporting Information), two characteristic peaks at 234 and 333 cm<sup>−1</sup> assigned to the stretching vibrations of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>S bond are observed, confirming the formation of TiS<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0027_R_d238440625e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">27</a></span> Furthermore, the morphology and microstructure of the as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> are observed by scanning and transmission electron microscopy (SEM/TEM). As shown in the SEM image (Figure S3a,b, Supporting Information), the products show a micrometer-sized flake-like architecture. The TEM image (Figure S3c, Supporting Information) exhibits a typical layered structure with a lateral dimension of 50 nm. The high-resolution (HR)-TEM image of TiS<sub>2</sub> (Figure S3d, Supporting Information) shows the characteristic lattice fringes of the (101) plane with a spacing of 0.262 nm, consistent with the X-ray diffraction (XRD) results. Energy dispersive spectroscopic mapping suggests that Ti and S species are distributed homogeneously throughout the particle (Figure S4, Supporting Information). Benefiting from the micrometer-sized structure, as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> presents a relatively high tapped density of 1.55 g cm<sup>−3</sup>, which can be packed together densely and is favorable for practical applications.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs798-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d2288e1b-9c08-42e8-b296-57b3b0dd8e5b/advs798-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d2288e1b-9c08-42e8-b296-57b3b0dd8e5b/advs798-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/11797ea4-a75c-47f9-98d2-1aaf421be494/advs798-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d2288e1b-9c08-42e8-b296-57b3b0dd8e5b/advs798-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs798-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801021" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Crystal structure, b) XRD pattern, c) Ti 2p, and d) S 2p XPS spectra of TiS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The Na-storage behavior of as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> was evaluated by both cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling at room temperature. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs798-fig-0002">2</a>a,b displays the CV curves of the as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode in a typical ether-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME) and ester-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC) within the potential window of 0.3–3 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). In the CV curves of the electrode in the ether-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME), there are several reduction peaks centered at 2.0, 1.6, 1.4, and 0.78 V in the first reduction scan, corresponding to the anodic peaks at 1.5, 1.7, 1.9, and 2.2 V, respectively. These redox peaks are ascribed to the multiple steps of intercalation and conversion reactions of layered TiS<sub>2</sub>. The peak located at 0.42 V, which disappears in the subsequent cycles, is ascribed to the irreversible deposition of the electrolyte and the formation of solid–electrolyte interface (SEI) films. Specifically, the CV currents increase gradually in the ensuing cycles, apparently involving an activation process of bulk TiS<sub>2</sub>. In contrast, as shown in Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0002">2</a>b, in the CV curves of the electrode in ester-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC), all the peaks in the first few scans visibly deteriorate, suggesting the unstable electrochemical reactions in the ester-based electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs798-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/047a104c-6661-45f3-ba61-bdf5600ff575/advs798-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/047a104c-6661-45f3-ba61-bdf5600ff575/advs798-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/12124191-8577-4e2a-8246-025f63b2243c/advs798-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/047a104c-6661-45f3-ba61-bdf5600ff575/advs798-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs798-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801021" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves of TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode in a) NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME and b) NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> in c) NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME and d) NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0002">2</a>c, d presents the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode in different electrolytes. In the ether-based electrolyte, TiS<sub>2</sub> delivers an initial discharge/charge capacity of 478.8/459.5 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a constant current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a high initial coulombic efficiency of 95.9%. Interestingly, the discharge capacity increases slowly to 1040 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and remain stable over 250 cycles. Based on the charge–discharge curves of the Ketchen black (Figure S5, Supporting Information), the capacity contribution from the carbon additives is estimated to be 49 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is negligible compared to the high reversible capacity of TiS<sub>2</sub>. It is noteworthy that the reversible capacity of TiS<sub>2</sub> is higher than the theoretical value of 957 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. The excess reversible capacity can be ascribed to the high reversible capacity of the S impurities (1672 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>). It should be pointed out the critical bottleneck for the development of metal sulfide anodes is the high charge/discharge voltage, which raise difficulties for their practical applications in constructing full batteries. However, for the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the capacity contribution in the low voltage range (0.3–1.0 V) is estimated to be ≈521 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, which is comparable to the values that reported for high-performance carbonaceous anodes and metallic alloys,<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0028_R_d238440625e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0032_R_d238440625e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">32</a></span> fulfilling the commercial demands for wide-spread applications. Remarkably, the charge/discharge curves corresponding to the 250th cycle demonstrate much smaller polarization compared to the initial curves, which can be ascribed to the gradual phase transformation. In contrast, the electrode cycled in the ester-based electrolyte (NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC) demonstrates an inferior initial coulombic efficiency of 48.2%. The reversible capacity fades rapidly from 846.2 to 59 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> in the first few cycles, which is in good accordance with the CV curves. The poor cycling stability in ester-based electrolyte can be ascribed to the side reactions between the carbonate solvents and the polysulfide.</p>
         
         <p>To further investigate the effect of the electrolyte, the electrochemical performances of TiS<sub>2</sub> are analyzed in different ether-based electrolytes with different Na salts and solvents (Figure S6, Supporting Information). Notably, the electrolyte cycled in all the ether-based electrolytes not only demonstrated a much better cycling stability but also an obviously higher initial coulombic efficiency of 72.3–95.9%. Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes after the first sodiation process in different electrolytes were recorded in the frequency range of 100 KHz–0.1 Hz. As shown in Figure S7 and Table S1 in the Supporting Information, the <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode in NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME, NaPF<sub>6</sub>/diglyme (DGME), NaPF<sub>6</sub>/tetraglyme (TGME), NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/DME, and NaClO<sub>4</sub>/DME are 4.3, 20.2, 27.4, 26.2, and 338.3 Ω, respectively, whereas that in NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC+DEC is 523.6 Ω, indicating the formation of a thinner SEI film and better ionic conduction of the ether-based electrolyte. In addition, the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of the electrode cycled in the ether-based electrolytes (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 12.7–32.7 Ω) is much lower than that cycled in the ester-based electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> = 50.5 Ω), suggesting that the ether-based electrolyte can effectively improve the electronic transfer kinetics. Apart from choosing an appropriate electrolyte, controlling the terminal voltage to an appropriate range is also crucial for enhancing the electrochemical performances of TMDs. For achieving optimized electrochemical performance, the cycling performance of the as-prepared electrode was evaluated over various voltage ranges, as shown in Figure S8 in the Supporting Information. When the voltage range is narrowed to 0.5–3.0 and 1.0–3.0 V, TiS<sub>2</sub> exhibits compromised reversible capacities of 469 and 327 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. However, when the voltage range is extended to 0.005–3.0 V, a serious capacity fading could be observed after 400 cycles. As a result, controlling the discharge voltage to 0.3 V provides the best electrochemical performance in terms of the reversible capacity and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <p>It is noteworthy that the reversible capacity increases continuously in the first several hundreds of cycles. To unveil the underlying reason for this continuous increase in capacity and gain insights into the reaction mechanism, ex situ XRD, TEM, and XPS characterizations were carried out to investigate the evolution of the electrode structure during cycling. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs798-fig-0003">3</a>a presents the typical charge/discharge profiles of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode for the initial and 30th cycle. During the first discharge process (I→II→III→IV), the XRD patterns of the electrode reveal the phase transformation from TiS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>0.22</sub>TiS<sub>2</sub> and NaTiS<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0003">3</a>b), indicating the step-wise intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the TiS<sub>2</sub>, which is consistent with the results reported previously.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0025_R_d238440625e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></span> Upon further discharge to 0.3 V, the characteristic peaks of layered Na-Ti-S gradually disappear and peaks at 30°, 30.9°, and 34.2° appear, which can be attributed to the structural transformation of layered Na-Ti-S to Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S. The HR-TEM image confirms the clear lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.234 nm at the state IV, corresponding to the (101) crystal planes of Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0003">3</a>d). Moreover, the binding energy of Ti 2p2/3 shifts from 458.9 to 458.3 eV in the high-resolution XPS spectrum of the electrode (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0003">3</a>e), indicating the reduction of TiS<sub>2</sub>. When recharged back to 3.0 V, the electrode reverts to its original state through the structural evolution generalized as Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S → NaTiS<sub>2</sub> → TiS<sub>2</sub>. Moreover, the charge and discharge products obtained after 30 cycles were also investigated. As shown in Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0003">3</a>c,e, the XRD and XPS results reveal the coexistence of Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S and metallic Ti at the discharged state corresponding to the 30th cycle. Compared to the initial cycle, the electrode is reduced to a lower valence state in the subsequent cycles, resulting in higher electrochemical utilization and reversible capacity. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations confirm that the reduced products, NaTiS<sub>2</sub> and Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S exhibit much higher electronic conductivity than pristine TiS<sub>2</sub> (Figure S9, Supporting Information). As a result, the formation of NaTiS<sub>2</sub>, Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S, and metallic Ti can provide a rapid kinetic pathway for the electronic transport in the interior of the electrode, leading to smaller polarization and higher electrochemical utilization of the active materials.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs798-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1a6bcf9f-3148-4484-a3c6-5ba4d7e7f60b/advs798-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1a6bcf9f-3148-4484-a3c6-5ba4d7e7f60b/advs798-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d7f869e7-0273-4381-9ad0-f09c3737ac4f/advs798-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1a6bcf9f-3148-4484-a3c6-5ba4d7e7f60b/advs798-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs798-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801021" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Ex situ characterization of TiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at different charge–discharge states: a) Charge/discharge profiles of the initial and the 30th cycle, b,c) XRD patterns, d) HRTEM images,and e) XPS spectra. The marks (I–IX) in (b)–(e) correspond to the marks in (a).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In addition to the structural transformation, morphological changes in the electrode were observed by ex situ SEM imaging. As shown in Figure S10 in the Supporting Information, the microsized TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode has pulverized to nanocrystals during the conversion reactions which involve repeated decomposition and reconstruction of the structure. For the bulk electrode, chemical conversion reactions are restricted to the surface of the particle owing to kinetic and thermodynamic limitations, resulting in inferior reversible capacities in the initial few cycles. When pulverized to nanosized particles, the nanoscale reaction domains can significantly overcome the kinetics and thermodynamic constraints and afford a rapid kinetic pathway for complete electrochemical transformations, leading to complete transformation from TiS<sub>2</sub> to metallic Ti and Na<sub>2</sub>S (Equations S1 and S2, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>The rate capability and long-term cycling stability of a battery are the key characteristics for their practical application. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#advs798-fig-0004">4</a>a exhibits the charge/discharge curves of the Na/TiS<sub>2</sub> batteries at different current densities ranging from 0.1 to 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> exhibits reversible capabilities of 1047, 1017, 853, 799, and 713 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.2, 1.0, 5, 10, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. More encouragingly, upon increasing the current density to 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 621 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> could still be maintained, indicating that the charge/discharge process can go to completion within 1 min. Compared to other TMDs, this TiS<sub>2</sub> demonstrates a remarkably fast charge–discharge capability, as summarized in Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0004">4</a>b.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0012_R_d238440625e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">12</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0015_R_d238440625e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0025_R_d238440625e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a>, <a href="#advs798-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0033_R_d238440625e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano">33</a>-<a href="#advs798-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0039_R_d238440625e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Energy Storage Mater.">39</a></span> CV measurements of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at different scanning rates were carried out to explain the high rate capability and cycling stability (Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0004">4</a>c,d). The charge storage mechanism always involves non-Faradaic and Faradaic processes. The capacitive effect of the battery system is calculated according to Equations S3 and S4 in the Supporting Information. The b-value is calculated to be ≈1 for all the peaks, which is a significant characteristic indicating that the Na/TiS<sub>2</sub> batteries are mainly governed by capacitance. The capacitive process and high conductivity enables a fast charge/discharge process, resulting in superior rate performance and an extended cycling life. Furthermore, the formation of highly conductive intermediate products (NaTiS<sub>2</sub>, Ti<sub>0.77</sub>S, etc.) is also significantly beneficial for the fast charge–discharge ability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs798-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/94542b3a-6865-4dfc-bdf2-ec1037968c57/advs798-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/94542b3a-6865-4dfc-bdf2-ec1037968c57/advs798-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f8825069-f223-4be3-a8c5-f322552ac61b/advs798-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/94542b3a-6865-4dfc-bdf2-ec1037968c57/advs798-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs798-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.201801021" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performances of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode. a) Charge/discharge profiles at various current rates ranging from 0.2 to 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b) comparison of the as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> with other sulfide materials of sodium ion batteries, c) CV curves of as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> after 100 cycles at different scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, d) log(i) versus log(v) plots, and e) long-term cycling performance.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The long-term cycling stability of the Na/TiS<sub>2</sub> battery was further evaluated at different current densities ranging from 5 to 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure S11 and S12 in the Supporting Information, after the activation process, the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode remains stable over 4000 cycles at different current densities ranging from 5 to 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>. More encouragingly, an impressive long-term cycling performance is achieved at the current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure <a href="#advs798-fig-0004">4</a>e, TiS<sub>2</sub> can deliver a capacity of 740 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and remain stable over 9000 cycles. The coulombic efficiency rapidly rise up to 99% in the first few cycles, indicating the high electrochemical reversibility of the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode at such ultra-high current density. Such excellent long-term cycling stability has been rarely reported in the previous literatures. Apart from the high chemical stability of the ether-based electrolyte, the impressive long-term cycling stability can also be ascribed to the strong chemisorption of TiS<sub>2</sub> on the polysulfide (Figure S13, Supporting Information). For most TMDs, the polysulfide generated during the long-term cycling dissolves in the ether-based electrolyte, leading to a poor cycling life. However, the TiS<sub>2</sub> electrode can entrap the polysulfides on its surface (Figure S14, Supporting Information), leading to a stable chemical composition of the electrode.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs798-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs798-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, TiS<sub>2</sub> is investigated as a conversion electrode for NIB in an ether-based electrolyte. Benefiting from the high conductivity and strong surface affinity to the polysulfide intermediates, as-prepared TiS<sub>2</sub> delivers a high reversible capacity of 1040 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, remarkable initial columbic efficiency of 95.9%, strong rate capability of 621 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 40 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and superior cycling stability over 9000 cycles. In addition, the nanosized particles generated during the conversion reaction can minimize the entropic barrier for the reversible conversion reaction, leading to high electrochemical utilization and superior cycling stability. This work may pave the way for the development of high-capacity conversion materials with stable cycling for prospective energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs798-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs798-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Materials Synthesis</i>: TiS<sub>2</sub> samples were synthesized by a solid-phase sintering method under vacuum. Typically, 0.1 g titanium and 0.3 g sulfur were mixed thoroughly, and then the mixture was heated under vacuum at 660 °C for 7 days. After cooling down, the brass power was centrifuged and washed with carbon disulfide for more than ten times to remove excess sulfur. Then the samples were dried at 80 °C under vacuum for 10 h to obtain the target material of TiS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The structure information was investigated by XRD (X'Pert PRO Diffractometer). The morphologies were observed by SEM (FEI Sirion 200) and TEM (JEOL JME-2010F). Raman spectra were collected on a LabRAM HR800 Raman system at a laser wavelength of 532 nm. XPS (AXIS-ULTRA DLD spectrometer) was carried out to investigate the elemental bonding configurations. S content was investigated by TG-DSC (Netzsch STA 449 F5) in an air atmosphere at a ramping rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: Except for using 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/DME as the electrolyte, the working electrode and coin cells were prepared as reported previously and the active material loading was ≈2.2–2.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>.<span><a href="#advs798-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs798-bib-0037_R_d238440625e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Mater. Chem. A">37</a></span> CV measurements and EIS were performed on a CHI 660D electrochemical workstation. The galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed on a LAND battery test system.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

All authors participated in the analysis and discussion of the results.</p>

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
